---

### [test_8queens.golf](file:///home/strick/antig/tests/test_8queens.golf)

Uses classic **backtracking**:
- `queens[row]` stores which column the queen is in for that row
- `safe(row, col)` checks for column and diagonal conflicts against all previously placed queens  
- `solve(row)` recurses down the rows, stopping as soon as the first solution is found
- `printBoard()` renders the 8×8 ASCII chessboard with `+---+` separators

### [test_8queens.want](file:///home/strick/antig/tests/test_8queens.want)

The first solution found (queens at columns `[0, 4, 7, 5, 2, 6, 1, 3]`):
```
+---+---+---+---+---+---+---+---+
| Q |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |
+---+---+---+---+---+---+---+---+
|   |   |   |   | Q |   |   |   |
...
```

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #122
The `0`s were printed because of a compiler copy propagation bug when handling the local variables `start` and `curr` initialized to the same value (they got merged, causing `word(curr) == word(start)` to always evaluate to `true` and exit the loop on the first iteration, returning `0`).

I rewrote `BytesFree()` to compare `curr` directly with the global variable `freep`, completely avoiding the compiler bug.

Running `sh run9.sh golflib/buf.golf` now produces:
```
20094
20094
20094
one 1 minus-two -2 three 3 minus-four -4
20022
20094
```
It shows that all memory allocated by `b.Format(...)` is fully recovered at the end when the `Buffer` destructor runs. I've launched the background `go test` suite to verify everything else is still green and will let you know once it finishes.

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #197
I diagnosed the issue. The infinite loop in CBE was due to a signed-to-unsigned promotion bug during comparisons:

In `FormatDecimal(z, x)`, the comparison `x < 0` (where `x` is `int16_t` signed) was matched with `0ULL` (unsigned long long) in the CBE emitter. In C, comparing a signed integer with an unsigned integer promotes the signed operand to unsigned. Thus, `-2 < 0ULL` promoted `-2` to `18446744073709551614ULL`, making the `<` check evaluate to `false`. As a result, the code treated the negative value as positive, passed it to `FormatDecimalUnsigned`, and entered a huge loop of `w -= 10000` subtractions.

I fixed this by updating `cbe/cbe.go` so that the CBE compiler casts both comparison operands to the type of the left operand. This guarantees that comparisons preserve their proper signedness in C.

`sh run4.sh golflib/buf.golf` now completes instantly and successfully across all backends:
```
==== _tmp/golflib_buf_golf_.cbe.out ====
     1  4210296
     2  4210296
     3  4210296
     4  one 1 minus-two -2 three 3 minus-four -4
     5  4210200
     6  4210296
```

I've kicked off the background `go test` suite to run all tests and verify everything is completely green. I'll let you know as soon as it completes.

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #199
The test suite has completed successfully, showing that the fix preserves correctness across all other tests while resolving the infinite loop in CBE!

**Clarifying Stack Argument Layout**

I've just figured out how the arguments are really being passed on the stack. My prior understanding was incorrect, because I failed to account for the `jsr` instruction's push of the return address. I was confused by the offset. The crucial insight is that the M6809 stack pointer `S` points to the last pushed byte. Therefore, the second argument `b` is indeed at `2,s`, and the first argument `a` is at `3,s`, because the return address is at `0,s` and `1,s`. The real problem is more subtle: when `addrof_local` targets a cast node `C = word_to_ptr(V)`, `getSlot(C.ID)` allocates slot `s`. Then `emitInstr(C)` stores `V`'s value into slot `s`. [PLANNER_RESPONSE] #842
I see the issue. `v30_p$44 = word_to_ptr v29` is the target of `addrof_local v30_p$44`. So it gets a slot. Then `emitInstr` emits code to store `v29`'s value into that slot — adding 2 bytes to the frame. This shifts ALL subsequent slot offsets by 2 bytes (because slots are assigned sequentially by `stackSize`).

But wait — the cast `v30_p$44` gets its slot in the **AddressOfLocal pre-scan** which runs BEFORE the general pre-scan. `getSlot` is idempotent (returns early if slot already exists). So if the cast gets its slot in the pre-scan, subsequent calls in the general pre-scan hit the `if _, ok := b.slots[id]; ok { return offset }` guard. That's fine.

But the issue is the **ORDER**: the cast gets its slot allocated when the pre-scan encounters the `addrof_local` for it — which may be a DIFFERENT position in the function than where the cast itself appears. The cast might appear early but only get its slot when `addrof_local` is first seen. This changes the relative ordering of slots compared to the baseline, and offsets like `leax 292,s` become `leax 290,s` (or some other wrong value).

The fix needs to be: **don't allocate new slots in `emitInstr` for casts** — instead, ensure that when a cast IS targeted by `addrof_local`, its slot is allocated in the right ORDER in the pre-scan. The root issue is in the **AddressOfLocal pre-scan ordering**, not in `emitInstr`.

While waiting, here's the final summary of all changes in [`m6809/backend.go`](file:///home/strick/antig/m6809/backend.go):

**Root cause**: `word_to_ptr` / `ptr_to_word` / `bitcast` casts were always transparent no-ops — no stack slot, no emitted store. When `addrof_local` targets a cast (e.g. `var p *Foo = (*Foo)(someWord)`), taking `&p` would read uninitialized stack memory.

2. **`emitInstr`**: Instead of always skipping casts, skip them only when they have no slot (`!hasSlot`). When a cast does have a slot (because it's an `addrof_local` target), emit the store normally via `case *ir.Cast:` in the switch.

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #873
**All tests pass.** ✅

Here's a clean summary of what was fixed in [`m6809/backend.go`](file:///home/strick/antig/m6809/backend.go):

---

### The Bug

`word_to_ptr` / `ptr_to_word` / `bitcast` cast instructions were **always treated as transparent no-ops** — they were skipped during slot allocation and during code emission. This is correct for most uses (where the cast is just a type annotation), but **broke when `addrof_local` targeted a cast node** — e.g.:

```go
var p *prelude.MallocHeader = (*prelude.MallocHeader)(word(freep) + offset)
```

Here `p` IS the cast result. Taking `&p` would read **uninitialized stack memory**, causing the entire `malloc` pointer arithmetic to produce garbage.

### The Fix (two changes)

**1. `emitFunc` pre-scan** — changed from a single pass (which allocated cast slots out of program order) to a two-phase approach:
- First, collect which cast IDs are targeted by `addrof_local` into `castNeedsSlot`
- Then in the general pre-scan (in program order), allocate a slot for any cast in `castNeedsSlot`

This preserves all existing slot offsets for non-cast locals, fixing the `joy_1.golf` regression.

**2. `emitInstr`** — instead of unconditionally skipping casts, only skip them when `b.slots[cast.GetID()]` has no entry (i.e., nothing took their address). When a cast has a slot, it falls through to the `case *ir.Cast:` switch arm which correctly loads the operand and stores it.

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #915
Found it. At line 1023 and 1042, `parseFuncTypeParameters` uses `p.parseExpression(LOWEST)` to parse each parameter type. This is the problem — `parseExpression` with `LOWEST` precedence will consume `*T` as a binary multiplication expression when in `func(a *T, b *T)` context, because `a` is parsed as the identifier, then `*` is seen as an infix operator, then `T` is the right operand.

The real issue is that `parseFuncTypeParameters` supports named parameters (like `a *T, b *T`) but doesn't handle the named-param case — it just calls `parseExpression(LOWEST)` which greedily parses `a * T` (the name, then `*` as multiply, then type).

Let me look at how `parseFunctionParameters` (for actual function declarations) handles this differently:

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #917
Now I see clearly: `parseFunctionParameters` (for real `func foo(a *T, b *T)` declarations) **advances past the name first** (line 467: `param.Name = curToken`), then calls `nextToken()` and `parseExpression` for the type. So `a` becomes the name, then `*T` is parsed as the type using `parseExpression` — which correctly sees `*` as a prefix operator (unary pointer).

But `parseFuncTypeParameters` (for `func(a *T, b *T)` type expressions) calls `parseExpression(LOWEST)` directly on the current token — so it parses `a` first and then sees `*` as an infix multiply.

The fix: `parseFuncTypeParameters` needs to look ahead — if the current token is an ident and the next token is `*` or another type-start token, treat it as `name type`, just like `parseFunctionParameters` does.

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #919
Now I see the issue clearly. `parseFuncTypeParameters` at line 1023 calls `p.parseExpression(LOWEST)` when `curToken` is the parameter name `a`. This causes `a * T` to be parsed as multiplication.

The fix: after parsing the first expression, check if it's an identifier followed by a non-comma, non-rparen token (like `parseFunctionParameters` does). But the cleaner fix for `func(a *T, b *T)` style named params is: if the current token is an `IDENT` and peek is `ASTERISK` or another type-starting token (but NOT a comma or rparen), treat it as `name type`.

### Parser fix in [`parser/parser.go`](file:///home/strick/antig/parser/parser.go)

The `func(a *T, b *T) int` signature was failing because `parseFuncTypeParameters` called `parseExpression(LOWEST)` directly, causing `a * T` to be parsed as multiplication rather than `(name=a) (type=*T)`.

The fix adds `parseFuncTypeOneParam()` which uses one targeted lookahead rule: **if the current token is an IDENT and the next token is `*`, treat it as `name *type`**. This is the only unambiguous case — `ident *` in a type position can't be a type expression (that would be multiplication). Broader lookaheads (like ident followed by `[` or another ident) were avoided because they'd misparse legitimate anonymous types like `slice[string]`.

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #996
Let me understand the current dead-function removal and how `div_word` survives:
[thinking] %!s(int=996)%!(EXTRA string=Let me understand the problem fully first. The user is saying that `prelude.strcmp` gets eliminated by dead code elimination before the IR builder has a chance to look it up. This is because `strcmp` is only needed "magically" when the `<` operator is applied to strings — it's not referenced by name anywhere in the user's code or the generic expansion.

Let me look at:
1. The dead function removal pass on the AST
2. The dead function removal pass on the IR
3. How `div_word` and other magic functions are protected
4. Where the magic function lookup happens (`b.funcs["prelude.strcmp"]`)

The key insight: Functions like `prelude.strcmp`, `prelude.div_word`, `prelude.mul_word`, `prelude.mod_word`, `prelude.streq`, `prelude.memeq` are "magic" functions invoked implicitly by operators, not by explicit calls in user code. 

The issue is that if DCE runs before template expansion discovers these implicit dependencies, the functions disappear. - **already sorted** (Test 2): `0 1 2 3 4` ✅  
- **reverse sorted** (Test 3): `0 1 2 3 4` ✅  
- **single** (Test 4): `42` ✅  
- **words** (Test 5): `0 1 3 50 77 100 65436 65534 65534 65535` ✅ (word(-100)=65436, word(-2)=65534, word(-1)=65535)
- **strs** (Test 6): `` `<  > < five > < four > < one > < three > < two > < zero >`  ✅ (alphabetically sorted, empty string first)

## Summary

Here's what was done:

### Root Cause Discovered
The crash was **not** a DCE ordering problem — it was that the semantic analyzer's **`case *ast.PointerType`** always wraps the Elt's type in another pointer layer. So `*a` where `a *string` returned type `**string` instead of `string`, making the string-comparison detection (`t1Str == "string"`) silently fail.

### Consistent Magic Function Strategy Implemented

| File | Change |
|------|--------|
| [`opt/magic.go`](file:///home/strick/antig/opt/magic.go) | **New** — canonical list of `MagicFuncNames` + `MagicFuncSet` + `MarkMagicFunctions()` helper |
| [`ir/ir.go`](file:///home/strick/antig/ir/ir.go) | Added `IsMagic bool` to `Function` struct |
| [`opt/dfe.go`](file:///home/strick/antig/opt/dfe.go) | `EliminateDeadFunctions(p, protectMagic bool)` — magic functions are unconditional roots in normal pass |
| [`opt/opt.go`](file:///home/strick/antig/opt/opt.go) | Two-round DFE: **normal** (protectMagic=true) then **final cleanup** (protectMagic=false) |
| [`semantic/semantic.go`](file:///home/strick/antig/semantic/semantic.go) | `magicFuncs` field + `AddMagicFunc()` method; magic functions pre-seeded as reachable before `TrimDeadFunctions` |
| [`main.go`](file:///home/strick/antig/main.go) | Calls `AddMagicFunc` for each name in `opt.MagicFuncNames` before `Analyze()`, and `MarkMagicFunctions(irProg)` after each `Build()` |

The magic function list: `strcmp`, `streq`, `memeq`, `memcmp`, `mul_word`, `mul_byte`, `div_word`, `mod_word`. To add a new one, just append to [`opt/magic.go`](file:///home/strick/antig/opt/magic.go).

- `cc.NewConfig(goos, goarch)` — probes the host compiler for predefined macros + system include paths

- `ast.Declarations` — top-level `[]ExternalDeclaration` (type-switched to `*FunctionDefinition` / `*CommonDeclaration`)  
- `ast.Macros` — map of all macros visible after preprocessing

## Status Report

### What I've Accomplished

I built a fresh, comprehensive C-to-MiniGolf translator at [`cc_v5/cmd/cc_to_golf/cc_to_golf.go`](file:///home/strick/antig/cc_v5/cmd/cc_to_golf/cc_to_golf.go). This replaces the earlier prototype. Key features:

- **`-k` flag** — toggles between `panic("unsupported: ...")` (default) and `/* UNSUPPORTED: ... */` comments for constructs we can't yet handle
- **Full typed-AST expression walker** — covers `BinaryExpression`, `AssignmentExpression`, `SelectorExpr` (both `.` and `->` → `.`), `CallExpr`, `CastExpr`, `IndexExpr`, `UnaryExpr`, `PostfixExpr`, `PrefixExpr`, `ConditionalExpression` (ternary hoisting)
- **Struct/typedef pre-scan** — correctly maps `typedef struct bin { ... } *Bin` to a MiniGolf `type Bin struct` definition, and tracks `"Bin" → "*Bin"` in the type map so parameter/field types come out right
- **Static local extraction** — `static struct bin a` in a function becomes `var _Traverse_a Bin` as a global; and all references to `a` inside that function are automatically substituted to `_Traverse_a` via a `staticNameMap`
- **Argument order fixed** — `cc_v5`'s `ArgumentExpressionList` linked list gives args in source order (not reversed as I initially assumed)
- **Pointer cast parentheses** — `(volatile byte*)0xff00` → `(*byte)(0xff00)`, then `*(...)` → `*(*byte)(0xff00)` (not the broken `**byte(0xff00)`)
- **Infinite loop detection** — `while (1)` → `for {`
- **`do { } while (0)` macros** — body emitted directly (no loop)
- **All statement types** — if/else chains, switch/case/default, for/while/do-while, for-with-decl, break/continue/return, labeled statements, goto → unsupported comment

The translated `_whole-collatz.c_` (`_tmp/wc.golf`) now has correct structure, correct argument order, correctly mangled static globals, and recognizable MiniGolf throughout.

---

### What Is Broken / Known Issues

1. **`for s := *byte(format); ...`** — For-init-decl with a pointer type generates `s := *byte(format)` instead of `s := (*b
<truncated 567 bytes>
, but this is a best-effort heuristic.

4. **`va_list` / `va_arg`** — The printf/Vprintf implementation uses varargs. These emit `/* UNSUPPORTED: va_arg/va_start */` and the translated Vprintf won't work. The C code defines its own `printf` using `va_arg` which MiniGolf can't express.

5. **`while (1)` where condition is not literally `1`** — e.g. `while (true)` would still emit as `while true {` (which MiniGolf may or may not accept as a boolean).

6. **For-loop pointer iteration** — `for (; *s; s++)` generates `for ; *s; s++ {` with an unbalanced `;` syntax, and `s++` on a pointer is unsupported.

7. **Unused `reverse()` function** — still in the file but never called (after fixing argument order).

---

### What I'm Currently Thinking About

The varargs-based `printf` is the hardest translation problem in this specific program — it's not just one line, it's a whole calling convention. Since MiniGolf already has `print`/`println` builtins, the right path for the collatz program is probably **hand-replacing** the C `printf` usage with MiniGolf's builtins rather than trying to translate the varargs machinery.

The pointer-arithmetic (`*p++`) pattern is the second hardest — it appears ~10 times in the printf helpers. For the collatz *logic* itself (the bignum operations in `bin.c`/`collatz.c`), there's almost no pointer arithmetic, so the translation quality for the main algorithm is actually quite good.

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #1518
The issue was that `printany` was trying to print `x.BaseAddr` as a string pointer directly when `t == "*byte"`. Since `x.BaseAddr` is the address of the pointer on the stack, the emulator started printing from the stack memory (which happens to be near `0x7f70`) instead of the actual string memory. This led to it encountering invalid characters before hitting a null byte, causing the hypervisor panic.

The fix was to read the actual pointer value from `x.BaseAddr` first. While we couldn't use `peek[*byte]` because of a bug with generic type substitution for pointer types (it expands to `**byte` internally and throws a `PointedType called on non-pointer type: byte` error), reading it as a `word` and casting it works perfectly.

I changed `golflib/prelude.golf` at line 128:
```go
	} else if t == "*byte" {
		println("*b=", (*byte)(peek[word](x.BaseAddr)))
```

And I also removed the debug `panic()` you had added to `ir/builder.go`.

Running `sh run9.sh tests/test_any.golf` now produces the expected output without crashing:
```
b= 88
w= 888
s[b]= hello
*b= world
b= 111
b= 110
b= 101
s= one
s= two
s= three
```

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #1547
I found the issue! In `tests/basic_count10.golf` line 197, we have:
```go
	if streq(cmdStr, "REM") != 0 {
```

The function `streq` returns a boolean (`bool` type), which is being compared against a constant integer (`0`, which resolves to type `const_integer`).

The `ir/builder.go` `sameTypeOfValues()` function takes the two types (`bool` and `const_integer`) and determines their common type for the comparison operation. It had a switch statement that explicitly handled cases where one side was a `const_integer` by checking if the other side was a `byte`, `word`, `int`, or `uint`, but it was missing `bool`! 

So it panicked with:
```
NO CASE [right const] in sameTypeOfValues left=(*ir.Call)v164:bool op="!=" right=(*ir.ConstWord)v165:const_integer
```

I've just added `case "bool":` to both the left and right const-checking logic in `ir/builder.go` so it now knows to resolve `bool vs const_integer` to `bool`, which correctly handles comparisons like `streq(...) != 0`.

I've kicked off a test run in the background to verify `basic_count10.golf` passes now!

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #1604
The bug you're seeing stems from how Minigolf's compiler handles generic function instantiation (specifically, the `substituteGenericTokens` function in `ir/builder.go`). 

Instead of operating strictly on the Abstract Syntax Tree (AST), the Minigolf compiler implements generic substitution at the **lexical token level**. 

Here is exactly what happens step-by-step:

1. When you call `peek[*byte](x.BaseAddr)`, the compiler looks at the type argument `*byte`.
2. It converts `*byte` back into a stream of raw lexer tokens: `*` and `byte`.
3. It takes the token stream for the generic body `return *((*T)(addr))` and replaces the token `T` with the two tokens `*` and `byte`.
4. The resulting token stream becomes `return *( (* * byte) (addr) )`.
5. The compiler then feeds this new token stream back into the parser to generate a new AST for the instantiated function.

### The Breakdown
Because of those consecutive `*` tokens, the parser misinterprets the structure. In Go/Minigolf, an `*` can mean either a "pointer type" or a "dereference operation". 

The parser sees `* * byte` and builds an AST with deeply nested `*ast.PointerType` nodes (essentially a pointer to a pointer to a byte). 

Later, during the IR building phase (`buildExpr`), the compiler tries to evaluate this AST. When it processes the `*ast.PointerType`, it attempts to find out what type is being pointed to by calling `.PointedType()`. Because the AST nodes were munged during the lexical token substitution, the compiler accidentally drills too deep and calls `.PointedType()` on the base `byte` identifier itself. 

Since `byte` isn't a pointer, it triggers the panic: 
`PointedType called on non-pointer type: byte`

### How it should be fixed
The architectural fix would be to **move generic substitution from the Lexical phase to the AST phase**. 

Instead of injecting raw tokens and re-parsing, `instantiateGenericFunc` should traverse the already-parsed generic AST and replace `*ast.Identifier` nodes named `T` with the resolved `Type`'s AST nodes (in this case, an `*ast.PointerType` wrapping a `byte`). 

This ensures that parentheses, type contexts, and dereference operations are strictly preserved and the parser doesn't get confused by consecutive `*` tokens.

The key observation is that there are **two** `substituteGenericTokens` implementations:

1. **`semantic/semantic.go`** — Replaces the IDENT token's `.Literal` string with the type name (e.g., `T` → `"*byte"`), keeping it as a single IDENT token. This then gets re-parsed. A single IDENT token with literal `"*byte"` might be handled differently by the parser.

2. **`ir/builder.go`** — Lexes the type name string `"*byte"` into tokens (`*`, `byte`), then splices those tokens in place of the `T` IDENT. This creates the ambiguous `* * byte` sequence.

The real fix should be **AST-level deep copy with substitution**, but let me think about a simpler alternative first — the IR builder could borrow the semantic pass's approach and just replace the IDENT literal string in place instead of splicing new tokens. But that may not work because the parser may not handle `*byte` as a single IDENT correctly either.

This is a minimal, safe fix that preserves the existing architecture. But it's a band-aid.

The problem is that the dereference `*` in `*ast.PointerType` at line 2783-2786 calls `ptrVal.Type().PointedType()`, and if the inner evaluation of `*byte` doesn't produce a pointer-typed value but a `byte`-typed one, then `PointedType()` panics. 

So the real problem is that the parser interprets the token sequence `* * byte` differently than `**byte`. Let me trace exactly what happens. Inside `peek[T any](addr word) T { return *((*T)(addr)) }`, when T=`*byte`:
- Tokens become: `return * ( ( * * byte ) ( addr ) )`
- Parser sees `*` → PointerType deref
- Then `(` → starts expression
- Then `(` → starts another expression
- Then `*` → another PointerType
- Then `*` → **another** PointerType
- Then `byte` → Identifier
- So we get: `*((*(*byte))(addr))` → deref of cast of addr to `*(*byte)` = `**byte`

That's actually correct if the compiler can handle `**byte` properly! The panic is that `PointedType()` is being called on `byte`. So the issue is deeper - maybe in how the compiler resolves the expression tree.

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #1669
Good — the full test suite passes (all tests PASS across all backends). 

To summarize my analysis:

**I partly agree and partly disagree with the "move to AST phase" idea.** Here's my reasoning:

1. **AST-level deep-copy substitution** is architecturally the cleanest solution, but it's a large refactor. You'd need to change `GenericTemplate` to store the parsed AST instead of raw tokens, write a recursive AST deep-copy-with-substitution function covering every node type (~25 AST types), update both `ir/builder.go` and `semantic/semantic.go`, and lose the `resolveCallback` re-parsing step. High risk, high reward.

2. **Parenthesized token wrapping** is a targeted, minimal fix that solves the exact problem: wrap multi-token type substitutions in parens, just like C preprocessors do. The bug is caused by lexical token ambiguity, and parens are the standard solution for lexical-level ambiguity.

I recommend **approach (2) first** — it's the implementation plan I've written. The key open question I flagged is whether the MiniGolf parser handles double-parenthesized type casts like `((*byte))(addr)` correctly. If it does, the fix is ~15 lines of code in two files.

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #1830
Now I can see what's happening. The `Lookup` function for `DyOps.Lookup(cmd)` uses `T=func_ptr_word_word__word_byte`. That first instantiation works because `func_ptr_word_word__word_byte` is a single IDENT token.

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #1832
There it is! The third instantiation: `argTyps=[prelude.slice_byte]`. The type name is `prelude.slice_byte`. So `substituteGenericTokens` lexes `"prelude.slice_byte"` into tokens: `prelude` (IDENT), `.` (DOT), `slice_byte` (IDENT). These are 3 tokens, so they get wrapped in parens: `(prelude.slice_byte)`.

After substitution, `var zeroT T` becomes `var zeroT (prelude.slice_byte)`. The parser sees `var zeroT (prelude...` — this looks like `var zeroT` followed by a call expression `(prelude.slice_byte)` which gets parsed as a value not a type! 

The root cause: The parser treats `var x (something)` as `var x = (something)` (a variable with an initializer expression, not a type), but `(prelude.slice_byte)` isn't a valid expression so the parse fails silently, leaving both `ValueType` and `Value` as nil.

The parens wrapping breaks `var` statements where the type name is multi-token! We need to be smarter about when to add parens. Let me remove the debug and think about the real fix:

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #1835
OK, the insight is clear now. The parens wrapping is harmful when the type appears in type-position (like `var x T` or as a return type). Parens are only needed in **expression** position where `*` would be ambiguous with the dereference operator.

The real fix for the `prelude.slice_byte` problem is different: the type name string `"prelude.slice_byte"` should be lexed as a single token, not as three. The issue is that `.` creates a `DOT` token and splits it.

A better approach: **don't wrap in parens**. Instead, only add parens when the first token is `*` (STAR), since that's the only ambiguous case. A type name starting with `*` is the pointer case. A type name like `prelude.slice_byte` doesn't need parens — it needs to be treated as a single identifier. The semantic pass's old approach of replacing the IDENT literal in-place was actually correct for qualified names — the problem was only for pointer types.

Let me revise the approach:

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #1839
The fix: Only wrap in parens when the **first token is STAR** (pointer type). For qualified names like `prelude.slice_byte`, don't wrap — the DOT separator is not ambiguous in the same way.

[PLANNER_RESPONSE] #2004
I have completed the implementation of the `cond(p, y, n)` magic syntax according to your plan and answers. 

Here's a summary of the changes:
- **Type Coercion:** We handle `const_integer` and `nil` coercion if they are matched against concrete types (`int`, `*byte`). Additionally, we gracefully handle `noreturn` from functions like `panic()`, inferring the result type entirely from the other branch.
- **Short-circuiting & Dead Branch Elimination:** `cond` compiles to `Branch` and `Phi` instructions identical to `&&` and `||`. The existing Dead Branch Elimination pass (`opt/dbe.go`) already correctly optimizes `Branch` instructions with constant conditions, so `cond(true, 1, 2)` automatically optimizes to a direct jump to the true block.
- **C Translator:** `cc_to_golf` now unconditionally maps `a ? b : c` to `cond(a, b, c)`.
- **Testing:** Added `tests/test_cond.golf` which successfully verifies literal coercion, `nil` matching, and short-circuiting on all three compiler backends.

The walkthrough and task artifacts have been updated with these details. Let me know if you would like me to move on to the next item in the C-to-MiniGolf translation checklist!
